United States of America
Regulation Status
❗️ This tracker only tracks developments at the US federal level. For US state-based developments, see suggested resources in the "Further Reading" section.
Various federal laws, bills, executive orders and frameworks on AI have been passed or proposed on a range of matters (from strategic or administrative initiatives to regulation of specific AI issues or applications such as deepfakes and foundation models).
In addition to federal initiatives, US efforts in AI regulation and legislation are fragmented across local and state levels too. The states of California and New York tend to lead the other states in terms of volume of proposed bills. However, Executive Order 14365 ("Ensuring a National Policy Framework for Artificial Intelligence" signed in December 2025 orders the establishment of an AI Litigation Task Force within the Department of Justice to challenge statutes on grounds that these state AI laws unduly burden interstate commerce.
Legislative themes in the US tend to relate to national security, bias and fairness, transparency and disclosure, and accountability.
Latest Macro Developments

Filter Categories...
[8 May 2026] White House distances itself from tighter AI regulation:
It is reported that the White House has distanced itself from tighter AI regulation, emphasizing a desire for partnership with tech companies rather than government oversight, despite discussions about a vetting system for AI models from firms like OpenAI, Anthropic, and Google. Senior officials, including Kevin Hassett, indicated that a pre-release safety testing regime similar to FDA drug approvals is being considered, which has raised concerns in the tech industry about potential competitive disadvantages. The administration is also contemplating involving the intelligence community to assess AI models before their public release to mitigate risks from adversaries. However, industry leaders worry that mandatory pre-release oversight could stifle innovation and competition. The situation is further complicated by tensions between the Pentagon and Anthropic regarding the use of its AI models, as well as the administration's urgent response to emerging AI capabilities that could pose security threats.

Defense & Security
Public Sector & Governance
[30 April 2026] Workforce Transparency Act introduced:
U.S. Senators Mark R. Warner (D-VA) and Ted Budd (R-NC) have introduced the Workforce Transparency Act, which mandates the Department of Labor to collect and publicly disseminate data on AI usage in both public and private sectors to provide policymakers, researchers, businesses, and government entities with timely information to understand and address AI's impact on the U.S. workforce, ultimately guiding the development of evidence-based solutions to workforce challenges posed by AI integration.

Labor & Workforce
Data Privacy & Protection
Public Sector & Governance
[28 April 2026] CHATBOT Act introduced:
U.S. Senate Commerce Committee Chairman Ted Cruz (R-Texas) and Senators Brian Schatz (D-Hawaii), John Curtis (R-Utah), and Adam Schiff (D-Calif.) have introduced the CHATBOT Act, legislation aimed at giving parents control over their children's interactions with AI chatbots, addressing concerns about risks such as exposure to inappropriate content and manipulative design features. The bill mandates AI companies to create "family accounts" for parental management, requires parental consent for chatbot usage, and prohibits targeted advertising to minors, while also calling for further research on potential harms associated with chatbots. The bill has garnered support from various organizations advocating for responsible innovation and child safety.

Online Safety & Child Protection
Generative AI
[28 April 2026] US senator Bernie Sanders urges international cooperation to halt AI’s ‘runaway train’:
It is reported that US senator Bernie Sanders (I-Vt.) has emphasized the necessity of international cooperation to regulate AI during a panel discussion on Capitol Hill with leading Chinese scientists, warning of societal risks such as misinformation, data privacy loss, and increased adolescent isolation due to chatbot dependency. Sanders expressed concerns about potential unemployment from automation and the dangers of super-intelligent systems operating beyond their creators' control, likening the current AI development to a "runaway train" lacking brakes. Sanders proposed an international treaty akin to the cold-war nuclear pact to ensure safety measures are implemented, despite some conservative backlash regarding collaboration with Chinese academics. Sanders has previously advocated for a halt on new AI datacenter constructions, asserting that Congress must catch up with the rapid technological changes brought by AI and robotics.

Human Rights & Ethics
Defense & Security
[23 April 2026] Economy of the Future Commission Act introduced:
Congressman Jay Obernolte (CA-23) and Congresswoman Sara Jacobs (CA-51) have introduced the Economy of the Future Commission Act of 2026, bipartisan legislation aimed at preparing the US for the economic impacts of AI, which is the House companion to a Senate bill by Senators Mark Warner and Mike Rounds. This legislation seeks to establish a bipartisan, bicameral commission to study AI's transformation of the American economy and develop policy recommendations for Congress, focusing on workforce development, education, federal AI adoption, and U.S. competitiveness in emerging technologies. The commission is tasked with delivering an interim report within seven months on AI's projected impacts on jobs and the economy, followed by a final report within 13 months that includes legislative recommendations on workforce reskilling, tax policy, unemployment insurance, and long-term economic growth.

Labor & Workforce
[23 April 2026] OSTP issues memorandum alleging foreign entities are conducting coordinated campaigns to distill US frontier AI systems:
The Office of Science and Technology Policy (OSTP) has issued a memorandum titled "Adversarial Distillation of American AI Models" (NSTM-4), signed by Michael Kratsios, Assistant to the President for Science and Technology and Director of the OSTP, and addressed to the Heads of all Executive Departments and Agencies. The memorandum states that the US government has evidence that foreign entities, primarily in China, are conducting coordinated, industrial-scale campaigns to steal American frontier AI systems through distillation attacks — a technique that involves training a smaller AI model by systematically harvesting the outputs of a larger, more advanced one. These campaigns reportedly use tens of thousands of proxy accounts and jailbreaking techniques to evade detection, and while the resulting models may not fully replicate the originals, they can appear to perform comparably on select benchmarks at a fraction of the development cost. The memorandum also warns that such attacks can be used to deliberately strip security safeguards from AI models, removing controls designed to keep them ideologically neutral and truth-seeking. In response, the administration stated it will share information with US AI companies about the tactics and actors involved, work with private industry to develop defenses against large-scale distillation campaigns, and explore measures to hold foreign actors accountable, while noting that lawful distillation for open-source development remains a legitimate practice.

Compute
Copyright & IP
[26 March 2026] House Committee Passes Chip Security Act:
The House Foreign Affairs Committee has passed bipartisan legislation, the Chip Security Act, aimed at preventing the smuggling of US semiconductors to foreign adversaries, particularly in response to concerns raised by the Select Committee on China regarding the misuse of advanced chips by companies like Deepseek. The Act mandates location verification for advanced AI chips, requires chipmakers to report potential diversions of sensitive technology, and calls for the Department of Commerce to study additional safeguards against the theft and misuse of US chips. This legislation is a direct response to recent indictments by the Department of Justice against individuals involved in smuggling AI chips to China, highlighting the national security threat posed by such activities and reinforcing the need for protective measures to maintain America's technological edge over China.

Chips & Data Centres
[25 March 2026] AI Data Center Moratorium Act introduced:
US senators Bernie Sanders (I-Vt.) and Rep. Alexandria Ocasio-Cortez (D-N.Y.) have introduced the Artificial Intelligence (AI) Data Center Moratorium Act which proposes a nationwide moratorium on the construction of new AI data centers until comprehensive national safeguards are established. The bill aims to ensure that the economic benefits of AI and robotics extend to workers rather than just wealthy Big Tech owners, asserting that AI does not increase utility prices or harm communities or the environment. Furthermore, the bill seeks to halt a global competition to eliminate jobs or create destructive AI by banning US exports of AI computing infrastructure to countries lacking adequate safeguards for safety, worker protection, and environmental preservation.

Chips & Data Centres
[25 March 2026] NSF initiative aims to make every American worker, business and community AI-ready:
The U.S. National Science Foundation (NSF) has launched the NSF TechAccess: AI-Ready America initiative to enhance AI literacy and application across the nation. This program aims to provide all Americans with the knowledge, tools, and training necessary to engage with the AI economy. In collaboration with federal partners, including the Department of Agriculture's National Institute of Food and Agriculture, the Department of Labor, and the Small Business Administration, NSF is offering funding opportunities to establish AI-ready Coordination Hubs in every U.S. state and territory. This initiative seeks to bridge the gap between the country's AI capabilities and the workforce, businesses, and communities that can benefit from them.

Education & Training
[25 March 2026] Youth AI Privacy Act introduced:
US senator Edward J. Markey (D-Mass.) has introduced the Youth AI Privacy Act, aiming to protect minors from privacy and safety risks associated with AI chatbots. The legislation mandates clear disclosures that chatbots are not human, restricts the use of minors' personal data for training AI models, bans advertisements targeting minors, and prohibits features that encourage excessive use, such as push alerts. The Federal Trade Commission, state attorneys general, and private plaintiffs are authorized to enforce these provisions.

Data Privacy & Protection
Online Safety & Child Protection
[25 March 2026] President Trump announces members of Council of Advisors on Science and Technology:
President Trump has appointed the first members to his President’s Council of Advisors on Science and Technology (PCAST), established by Executive Order to advise the President on enhancing American leadership in science and technology. Co-chaired by David Sacks and Michael Kratsios, the initial appointees include Marc Andreessen, Sergey Brin, Safra Catz, Michael Dell, Jacob DeWitte, Fred Ehrsam, Larry Ellison, David Friedberg, Jensen Huang, John Martinis, Bob Mumgaard, Lisa Su, and Mark Zuckerberg. PCAST will address the opportunities and challenges posed by emerging technologies to the American workforce, aiming to ensure that all Americans benefit during the Golden Age of Innovation. The Council may consist of up to 24 members, with additional appointments and details about the first meeting to be announced. Each President since Franklin D. Roosevelt has established a similar advisory committee.

Public Sector & Governance
R&D
[20 March 2026] White House unveils national AI legislative framework:
The Trump Administration has issued a comprehensive national legislative framework aimed at winning the AI race to enhance human flourishing, economic competitiveness, and national security for Americans, while addressing public concerns about the technology's impact on issues such as children's wellbeing and energy costs, outlining 7 key objectives: (1) protecting children in AI services, (2) strengthening communities and small businesses through AI development, (3) safeguarding American creators from AI-generated content infringement, (4) defending free speech and First Amendment protections against AI censorship, (5) leading global AI innovation by removing barriers and ensuring access to testing environments, (6) ensuring American workers benefit from AI-driven growth through skills training and job creation, and (7) establishing a federal AI policy framework to protect rights and support innovation while preventing a fragmented state regulation landscape.

Public Sector & Governance
Framework
Strategy
[20 March 2026] US House Democrats introduce bill to block Trump order targeting state AI laws:
U.S. Representatives Don Beyer (D-VA), Doris Matsui (D-CA), Ted Lieu (D-CA), Sara Jacobs (D-CA), and April McClain Delaney (D-MD), have introduced the Guaranteeing and Upholding Americans’ Right to Decide Responsible AI Laws and Standards (GUARDRAILS) Act to repeal President Trump's executive order aimed at establishing a moratorium on state-level AI policies, which they argue undermines state efforts to implement necessary safeguards against the risks of AI technologies. The legislation seeks to prevent the executive order from taking effect, allowing states to enact protections related to algorithmic bias, data privacy, and consumer safety, while Senator Brian Schatz is introducing a companion bill in the Senate. The representatives criticize the executive order for lacking federal protections and for attempting to limit Congressional regulatory action, asserting that states must retain the ability to protect the public in the absence of meaningful federal regulations. The bill is co-sponsored by several other Democratic representatives, emphasizing the need for a collaborative approach to establish a national AI framework that prioritizes public safety and innovation.

Federal v State
[18 March 2026] Blackburn releases discussion draft of TRUMP AMERICA AI Act:
Senator Marsha Blackburn has released a discussion draft of the TRUMP AMERICA AI Act, aiming to establish a unified federal framework for AI regulation. This proposed legislation seeks to protect children, creators, conservatives, and communities from potential AI-related harms while promoting US leadership in AI innovation. Key provisions include imposing a duty of care on AI developers to prevent foreseeable user harm, sunsetting Section 230 protections, mandating safeguards for minors on online platforms, and holding AI system developers accountable for damages caused by their systems. The act also incorporates elements from previously introduced bipartisan bills, such as the Kids Online Safety Act and the NO FAKES Act, to enhance protections for children and creators.

Online Safety & Child Protection
Liability & Accountability
[17 March 2026] Artificial Intelligence Ready Data Act introduced:
US senators Ted Budd (R-N.C.) and Andy Kim (D-N.J.), members of the Senate Commerce, Science, and Transportation Committee, have introduced the Artificial Intelligence Ready Data Act, which would create a modern federal approach to opening government data assets to train and develop stronger US AI models. Recognizing the vast and sophisticated data assets maintained by the US government, the bipartisan legislation seeks to establish clear standards to ensure these datasets are AI-ready, thereby supporting scientific breakthroughs and maintaining transparency. The bill has garnered endorsements from major organizations, including Google, Siemens, Snorkel AI, and the NC Chamber, all emphasizing the importance of high-quality, accessible government data in advancing trustworthy AI systems.

Public Sector & Governance
[16 March 2026] White House and House GOP prepare to block state AI laws:
It is reported that the White House and House GOP are collaborating on a legislative package to establish a national standard for AI regulation, aiming to preempt state-level AI laws. This initiative seeks to streamline AI governance and includes provisions for children's online safety, such as the NO FAKES Act, which addresses unauthorized AI-generated replicas of performers. Previous attempts to pass similar preemption measures have faced challenges, and the current effort aims to garner broader bipartisan support. AI companies advocate for this federal standard to avoid the complexities of varying state regulations, which they argue hinder innovation. However, the proposal faces opposition from some lawmakers concerned about safety and the preservation of states' rights.

Public Sector & Governance
Federal v State
Online Safety & Child Protection
[11 March 2026] Data center moratorium gains traction among Capitol Hill progressives:
It is reported that a proposal for a nationwide moratorium on new data centers is gaining support among progressive lawmakers, led by Vermont independent Senator Bernie Sanders, following advocacy from groups like Food and Water Watch citing environmental concerns. While the idea remains contentious across party lines, lawmakers such as Rep. Maxwell Frost (D-Fla.) and former Progressive Caucus Chair Pramila Jayapal (D-Wash.) argue it would allow regulators to catch up with rapid development, emphasizing the need for legislative oversight. However, some Democrats, including Senator John Fetterman (D-Pa.), oppose the moratorium, fearing it could hinder U.S. competitiveness in AI. Local initiatives, like a proposed three-year moratorium in New York and a review plan in Denver, reflect growing concerns about the energy and water demands of data centers. Despite the buzz, a POLITICO poll indicates that half of voters support building more data centers, with opposition rising when proximity to residences is considered. The White House is encouraging companies to manage their energy sourcing amid these discussions, while some Republicans criticize the moratorium as detrimental to growth, with varying responses from party members regarding the balance between community impacts and technological advancement.

Chips & Data Centres
[20 February 2026] White House science and technology policy director promotes AI adoption, sovereignty, and exports at India AI Impact Summit:
At the India AI Impact Summit 2026 in New Delhi, the White House Office of Science and Technology Policy Director Michael Kratsios has emphasized the US' commitment to AI leadership and international collaboration. Kratsios (1) highlighted America's AI Action Plan, focusing on innovation, infrastructure, and global partnerships, and announced initiatives like the American AI Export Program and the Tech Corps to support AI adoption worldwide; (2) advocated for national AI sovereignty, urging nations to adopt best-in-class technologies while developing local capabilities, and criticized global AI governance in favour of localized policies; and (3) addressed challenges in AI adoption, including trust, regulatory clarity, financing, and technical expertise, proposing U.S. support through various programs to overcome these barriers.

Trade & Investment
Sovereignty
[26 January 2026] Leveraging Artificial Intelligence to Streamline the Code of Federal Regulations Act introduced:
US Congressman Blake Moore (R-Utah) has introduced the draft text of "Leveraging Artificial Intelligence to Streamline the Code of Federal Regulations Act of 2026", aiming to streamline the Code of Federal Regulations (CFR) by using an AI tool to identify redundant and outdated rules. The proposed bill directs the Office of Management and Budget to implement an annual review process using AI, with agencies ultimately deciding whether to amend, rescind, or remove the identified rules. The initiative seeks to enhance efficiency without making automatic cuts, working in conjunction with agency personnel on recommendations. A companion bill was introduced in the Senate by a group of Republican senators, including John Husted (R-OH) and Joni Ernst (R-Iowa). This legislative effort aligns with the administration's push for increased AI adoption and a focus on efficiency within federal agencies.

Generative AI
Agentic AI
[21 January 2026] White House Council of Economic Advisers publish report on AI and the Great Divergence:
The White House Council of Economic Advisers has released a paper titled "Artificial Intelligence and the Great Divergence," drawing parallels between the transformative impact of AI and the Industrial Revolution's "Great Divergence," where industrializing nations experienced accelerated growth compared to others. The document highlights the US' leadership in AI investment, performance, and adoption, attributing this to the Trump administration's initiatives in fostering innovation, infrastructure development, deregulation, and promoting technology exports. The paper examines AI's potential effects on economic growth and the labor force, emphasising the rapid advancements in AI metrics, which are reportedly doubling every few months. It concludes by detailing President Trump's commitment to maintaining America's dominance in AI, underscoring the administration's proactive measures to secure this leadership position.

National Strategy
[15 January 2026] White House pledges to work with Congress on ‘sensible’ AI regulation:
At the Research and Technology Subcommittee Hearing on the "Advancing America’s AI Action Plan", US OSTP Director Michael Kratsios defended President Trump’s executive order seeking to block state AI rules, arguing that a fragmented state-by-state regulatory regime favoured major tech companies: “If you have this tremendous patchwork of laws all across the country, the folks who actually are able to work within that system most successfully are the deep-pocketed large, Big Tech companies". Director Kratsios did, however, agree with other congressional Republicans to commit to developing a "sensible" federal regulatory framework for AI that preempts certain types of state laws.

Antitrust & Competition
Sandbox
[12 January 2026] Remote Access Security Act passes in House of Representatives:
The US House of Representatives has passed the bipartisan Remote Access Security Act, which modernises the Export Control Reform Act by expanding federal authority to restrict foreign adversaries' remote access to critical technologies, including AI chips, through cloud computing services. This legislation clarifies that export controls apply equally to remote access, provision of access, and cloud-based exposure of controlled items, addressing concerns that Chinese-aligned entities use cloud services and remote computing to evade US restrictions on advanced semiconductors, AI, and other sensitive technologies. The bill now proceeds to the US Senate.

Defense & National Security
Cybersecurity
[8 January 2026] Industry standards critical to AI adoption, says US Chief Technology Officer:
It is reported that at the CES conference in Las Vegas, US Chief Technology Officer Ethan Klein said that establishing robust industry standards and regulatory frameworks is essential for boosting US competitiveness and ensuring the global adoption of AI and emerging technologies. By fostering trust through technical expertise—specifically via partnerships with organizations like NIST—the government aims to create a sustainable ecosystem that transitions these technologies from development to widespread market use. Ultimately, this standardized approach is designed to minimize deployment barriers, ensuring that technological progress consistently translates into economic prosperity and tangible benefits for the American public.

Sandbox
Cybersecurity
[19 December 2025] TRUMP AMERICA AI Act introduced:
US Senator Marsha Blackburn (R-Tenn.) has unveiled The Republic Unifying Meritocratic Performance Advancing Machine intelligence by Eliminating Regulatory Interstate Chaos Across American Industry (TRUMP AMERICA AI Act). This legislative framework would codify President Trump’s executive order to create one rulebook for AI that protects children, creators, conservatives, and communities from potential harms associated with AI, while promoting innovation to ensure the nation's leadership in the global AI race. Key provisions include imposing a duty of care on AI developers to prevent foreseeable harm, reforming Section 230 to enhance parental control over children's online access, safeguarding minors on online platforms, and protecting creators' rights by requiring explicit consent for the use of their data in AI training. Additionally, the act addresses concerns about bias against conservative viewpoints in AI systems and mandates transparency regarding AI's impact on employment.

Online Safety & Child Protection
Data Privacy & Protection
Copyright & IP
[18 December 2025] RESTRICT Act introduced:
Representative Gregory W. Meeks, Ranking Member of the House Foreign Affairs Committee, led 13 House Democrats in introducing the Restoring Export and Security Trade Restrictions for Integrated Circuit Technologies (RESTRICT) Act. This bill would prohibit the sale of the most advanced US AI chips to China and other countries of concern, while strengthening US competitiveness by creating a secure, license-free export pathway for trusted American companies operating data centres abroad. In particular, the bill would: (1) prohibit the sale of H200 and other advanced AI chips to China by directing the Under Secretary of Commerce to deny export licenses for advanced integrated circuits to countries of concern; (2) codify existing restrictions on exports of US advanced integrated circuits and related products to China without imposing new controls; (3) apply these prohibitions to all nations under a US arms embargo as of 1 January 2025; (4) safeguard national security while allowing export controls to adapt by permitting the Under Secretary of Commerce to revise the definition of “advanced integrated circuit or product” after two years with congressional certification; and (5) facilitate secure global operations for US firms by requiring regulations on physical, cybersecurity, and remote access security for license-free transfers to US-owned facilities outside countries of concern.

Cybersecurity
Defense & National Security
[17 December 2025] States' Right to Regulate Artificial Intelligence (AI) Act introduced:
The States' Right to Regulate Artificial Intelligence (AI) Act (SB 3557) was introduced in the Senate. The Act aims to prohibit the use of federal funds to implement, administer, or enforce the Executive Order Ensuring a National Policy Framework for AI, issued on 11 December 2025. That order seeks to establish a unified national AI policy framework designed to minimise regulatory burdens, preempt conflicting State laws, and balance innovation with protections for children, copyrights, free expression, and public safety. It directs federal agencies to challenge restrictive State AI laws, condition certain federal funding on State compliance, and implement federal standards for AI disclosure, reporting, and preemption.

Generative AI
[15 December 2025] OPM launches US Tech Force:
The US Office of Personnel Management (OPM), in coordination with the Office of Management and Budget (OMB), the General Services Administration (GSA), the White House Office of Science Technology and Policy (OSTP), and agency leaders across the administration, have announced the establishment of the United States Tech Force (Tech Force)– a new, cross-government program to recruit top technologists to modernize the federal government. The announcement follows the US AI Action Plan which calls for unleashing private sector innovation while also recognising the federal government must have the technical talent to lead in technology modernisation. Tech Force is a government-wide effort to surge teams of top engineers, data scientists, and technology leaders to tackle the government’s most complex and large-scale challenges and deliver on the President’s vision. In collaboration with leading private-sector technology companies, participants will receive world-class technical training and work closely with senior managers sourced directly from industry.

National Strategy
[12 December 2025] GUARD Act introduced:
US senators Ted Cruz (R-Texas) and John Cornyn (R-Texas) have introduced the Growing University AI Research for Defense (GUARD) Act. The bill would codify a portion of President Trump’s AI Action Plan and enable the Department of War (DoW) to establish and lead a National Security and Defense AI Institute to advance defense innovation, workforce development, and AI readiness. The proposed institute would facilitate collaboration between academia, government, and industry, focusing on defense-related AI data management and operational readiness to maintain the US' strategic advantage over foreign adversaries. Congressman Ronny Jackson (R-Texas-13) is leading companion legislation in the US House of Representatives, which passed earlier in the week as part of the National Defense Authorization Act.

Defense & National Security
[11 December 2025] President Trump signs executive order blocking states from regulating AI:
US President Donald Trump has issued an executive order to establish a unified national policy framework for AI, aiming to eliminate state-level regulations that hinder innovation and create compliance challenges for AI companies. The order directs the creation of an AI Litigation Task Force to challenge state laws deemed unconstitutional or obstructive, and requires the Commerce Department to evaluate state AI laws, identifying those that compel false outputs or violate constitutional rights. States with onerous AI laws risk losing federal funding under programs like BEAD, while agencies may condition grants on states refraining from enforcing such laws. The FCC is tasked with considering a federal reporting and disclosure standard, and the FTC must issue guidance clarifying that state laws mandating deceptive AI outputs are preempted. Finally, the order calls for legislative recommendations to establish a uniform federal AI framework, while preserving state authority in areas such as child safety, infrastructure, and procurement.

Generative AI
Online Safety & Child Protection
[11 December 2025] White House Office of Management and Budget adopted memorandum on increasing public trust in artificial intelligence through unbiased AI principles (M-26-04):
The Director of the Office of Management and Budget has issued a memorandum on increasing public trust in AI by setting out unbiased AI principles. The memorandum was in line with Section 4 of Executive Order 14319 to guide agencies in implementing AI principles and to complement earlier OMB guidance on AI acquisition. It applies to executive and military departments, independent establishments, and wholly owned government corporations, covering any large language model (LLM) procured by an agency and encouraging similar practices for national security systems where suitable. Agencies are required to include contractual obligations ensuring compliance with the two “Unbiased AI Principles” in all new solicitations and orders for LLMs and other relevant AI models issued after the memorandum date. The two Unbiased AI Principles require (1) that truth-seeking LLMs provide truthful responses to user prompts seeking factual information or analysis, prioritising historical accuracy, scientific inquiry, and objectivity, and acknowledging uncertainty where information is incomplete or inconsistent; and (2) LLMs must maintain ideological neutrality and operate as nonpartisan tools that do not shape responses to favour particular ideological positions. Developers are instructed not to intentionally encode partisan or ideological judgements into model outputs unless such content is expressly prompted by, or readily accessible to, the user. They are also expected to modify existing contracts for LLMs, where practicable, before extending performance. By 11 March 2026, agencies must update procurement policies to reflect these principles and establish mechanisms allowing users to report any violations. The memorandum sets out minimum transparency obligations for LLM vendors, including the provision of an Acceptable Use Policy, Model, System, or Data Cards, end-user resources, and a channel for feedback. Agencies should designate these obligations as material contract requirements to allow termination if a vendor fails to comply.

Generative AI
Defense & National Security
[10 December 2025] AI Talent Act introduced:
A bipartisan and bicameral group of legislators, including Rep. Sara Jacobs (CA-51), with Rep. Brown, Rep. Jay Obernolte (CA-23), and Rep. Pat Fallon (TX-04) as co-leads. Senator Andy Kim (D-NJ) and Jon Husted (R-OH), have introduced the AI Talent Act to enhance federal agencies' ability to recruit and retain top AI and technical talent. The proposed legislation aims to modernize federal hiring practices by establishing specialized talent teams, enabling skills-based hiring, and providing shared tools to expedite the identification and onboarding of qualified candidates. Key provisions include: (1) to establish Agency Tech & AI Talent Teams to improve recruiting, assessments, job announcements, and candidate evaluation; (2) to authorize OPM to create a central AI and tech talent team to lead pooled hiring, provide training and support to agency hiring teams, and develop shared resources; (3) to allow subject-matter experts to develop and administer skills-based assessments to ensure candidates demonstrate real technical proficiency; (4) to enable agencies to share qualified candidate pools (“shared certificates”), reducing duplicate hiring cycles and speeding placement; (5) to create a shared online platform to reuse and customize validated technical assessments across government; (6) to phase out automated self-assessments over five years, with limited, publicly posted waivers; (7) to allow agencies to stand up additional talent teams in other high-need areas (e.g., cybersecurity, data science, health care, IT); and (8) to authorize OPM to establish additional government-wide talent teams for high-need hiring priorities beyond AI and tech.

Cybersecurity
[9 December 2025] President Trump to issue order creating national AI rule:
In his post on Truth Social, US President Donald Trump said he would sign an executive order this week that would create a single national rule for AI, which the industry has said is necessary to override disparate laws passed by US states - "There must be only One Rulebook if we are going to continue to lead in AI... I will be doing a ONE RULE Executive Order this week. You can’t expect a company to get 50 Approvals every time they want to do something," wrote President Trump. President Trump did not provide details, though last month it was reported that the president was considering an executive order that would seek to preempt state laws on AI through lawsuits and by withholding federal funding.

National Strategy
[9 December 2025] President Trump greenlights Nvidia H200 AI chip sales to China:
In a post on Truth Social, President Trump said the US will allow Nvidia to ship its H200 AI chips to “approved customers” in China and elsewhere, under a set of conditions, and also said that Chinese President Xi Jinping “responded positively” to the proposal. Trump’s post appeared to state that 25% of the chips sales will be paid to the U.S. government as part of the deal, which the president said allows “for continued strong National Security.”

Defense & National Security
[9 December 2025] Digital Trade Promotion Act introduced:
US senators Todd Young (R-Ind.) led U.S. Senators Chris Coons (D-Del.), Jerry Moran (R-Kan.), and Michael Bennet (D-Co.) have introduced the Digital Trade Promotion Act to empower the president to negotiate and conclude digital trade agreements with other countries to regulate electronic commerce and cross-border data flows (broadly including AI). Key provisions include authorising the US Trade Representative to enter into comprehensive digital trade agreements, ensuring nondiscrimination for digital goods and services, prohibiting discriminatory digital taxes and data localization, and promoting cooperation in areas like AI and cybersecurity.

Data Privacy & Protection
Cybersecurity
Generative AI
[4 December 2025] SAFE Chips Act introduced:
US Senators Pete Ricketts (R-NE) and Chris Coons (D-DE) have introduced the Secure and Feasible Exports (SAFE) Chips Act of 2025, aiming to codify existing restrictions on the sale of advanced AI chips to countries like China, North Korea, Russia, and Iran. The bipartisan legislation seeks to maintain the United States' leadership in AI by preventing adversaries from accessing critical technologies, thereby safeguarding national security and economic interests. The bill would (1) direct the Secretary of Commerce to deny export license applications for advanced chips to foreign adversary countries for a period not less than 30 months; (2) after 30 months, the Executive Branch has discretion to update technical parameters as it sees fit; (3) define Democratic People’s Republic of Korea, Russia, Iran, and the People’s Republic of China, including Hong Kong and Macau, as Foreign Adversary Countries; (4) define advanced integrated circuits as any chips more powerful than those currently licensed for export to foreign adversary countries; and (5) direct the Department of Commerce to publish any proposed updates in the Federal Register that have been approved by a majority vote of the End-User Review Committee and to brief Congress 30 days before any proposed changes.

Defense & National Security
[3 December 2025] Republicans drop Trump-ordered block on state AI laws from defense bill:
It is reported that a recent attempt to include a ban on state-level AI regulation within an annual defence bill - the National Defense Authorization Act (NDAA) - has been rejected due to bipartisan opposition. House Majority Leader Steve Scalise indicated that Republican leaders, with President Trump's support, plan to seek alternative avenues to introduce this measure. This follows a previous unsuccessful effort to impose a 10-year moratorium on state AI laws, which faced strong resistance from both parties. Proponents from Silicon Valley argue that state regulations could create a complex patchwork hindering innovation, while critics contend that, in the absence of federal AI laws, preventing states from enacting their own regulations would leave Big Tech without sufficient oversight. Scalise acknowledged that the NDAA was not the appropriate vehicle for such a provision and echoed President Trump's suggestion to introduce the ban as a separate bill. A leaked draft executive order indicates that Trump is considering unilateral action, though these efforts are currently on hold (https://s3.documentcloud.org/documents/26287992/trump-executive-order-on-ai-law-preemption.pdf).

Defense & National Security
Antitrust & Competition
[3 December 2025] Nvidia CEO talks chip restrictions with Trump:
It is reported that Nvidia CEO Jensen Huang met with President Donald Trump to discuss US export controls on advanced AI chips, expressing concerns that these restrictions have significantly reduced Nvidia's market share in China. In the background, it is also reported that US lawmakers are considering to not include the GAIN AI Act in the annual defense package, known as the National Defense Authorization Act. The GAIN AI Act would have required chipmakers like Nvidia and Advanced Micro Devices to give US companies first pick on their AI chips before selling them in countries like China and other adversary nations (effectively limiting the company’s ability to sell chips to such markets).

Defense & National Security
[2 December 2025] AI Civil Rights Act to reintroduced:
US Senator Edward J. Markey (D-Mass.) and Representative Yvette Clarke (NY-09) have reintroduced the AI Civil Rights Act, aiming to prevent the use of biased and discriminatory AI algorithms in critical decision-making processes affecting American individuals. It has three key provisions: (1) to prohibit companies from offering, licensing, or using algorithms that discriminate on the basis of protected characteristics, like race or sex; (2) to require AI companies to conduct independent audits to identify and mitigate algorithmic discrimination; and (3) to give individuals the right to appeal an AI-based decision to a human, and empower the Federal Trade Commission, state attorneys general, and private individuals to enforce this part of the law. The bill was previously introduced in the US Senate in September 2024 but stalled in the 118th Congress session and expired thereafter.

Data Privacy & Protection
[25 November 2025] State lawmakers urge Congress to reject single, national AI policy:
In a letter to Congress, more than 200 state legislators, both Republican and Democratic, have called on Congress to reject a national defense bill provision that would strip their powers over AI. The provision, which would preempt existing state laws that regulate AI, is part of the National Defense Authorization Act. Key quotes: (1) "A blanket prohibition on state and local AI and automated decision-system regulation would abruptly cut off active democratic debate in statehouses and impose a sweeping pause on policymaking at the very moment when communities are seeking responsive solutions"; and (2) "We appreciate congressional engagement on AI and stand ready to collaborate on thoughtful national policy," they added. "But after years without comprehensive federal action on privacy and social media harms, a broad preemption of state and local AI laws until Congress acts would set back progress and undercut existing protections."

[22 November 2025] White House pauses executive order that would seek to pre-empt state laws on AI, sources say:
It is reported that, according to internal sources, the White House has paused a draft executive order intended to preempt state laws on AI by initiating lawsuits and withholding federal funds, indicating a period of policy uncertainty regarding AI regulation. The White House has not yet made an official comment.

National Strategy
[20 November 2025] Trump appears to ready executive order on state AI law preemption:
It is reported that President Donald Trump is considering an executive order aimed at preempting state-level AI regulations. The draft order proposes establishing a litigation task force to challenge state AI laws, restricting federal funding to states with AI statutes deemed "onerous," and directing federal agencies like the Federal Trade Commission and the Federal Communications Commission to develop federal standards that would override state regulations. The order specifically targets laws in California and Colorado, reflecting the administration's intent to centralize AI governance and prevent a patchwork of state regulations.

Cybersecurity
[19 November 2025] Advanced Artificial Intelligence (AI) Security Readiness Act introduced:
Senators Mark Kelly (D-AZ) and Todd Young (R-IN), members of the Senate Intelligence Committee, have introduced the Advanced Artificial Intelligence (AI) Security Readiness Act. The act would direct the NSA’s AI Security Center to develop and disseminate security guidance that identifies potential vulnerabilities and threats and establishes security strategies and contingency plans for America’s advanced AI systems. Companion legislation was introduced in the House of Representatives by Reps. Darin LaHood (R-IL-16), John Moolenaar (R-MI-02), Raja Krishnamoorthi (D-IL-08), and Josh Gottheimer (D-NJ-05).

Cybersecurity
Defense & National Security
[6 November 2025] US senators introduce resolution to affirm America’s artificial intelligence dominance:
US senators Chris Coons (D-Del.) and Tom Cotton (R-Ark.) introduced a bipartisan resolution emphasising the critical importance of maintaining America's leadership in AI to ensure national security and economic prosperity. The resolution highlights the necessity of preventing adversaries, particularly China, from surpassing the US in AI capabilities, given China's efforts to enhance its weapons systems and cyberattack potential. It acknowledges the US advantage in computing power, with American chipmakers producing significantly more advanced chips than their Chinese counterparts, and calls for continued efforts to restrict China's access to advanced chip technologies. The resolution also stresses the need for the US to build the most powerful supercomputers and next-generation AI models domestically, prioritize domestic companies' access to cutting-edge AI chips, and export the full US AI stack to allies while limiting access to the most advanced technologies for restricted countries.

Defense & National Security
[5 November 2025] AI-Related Job Impacts Clarity Act introduced:
US senators Josh Hawley (R-Mo.) and Mark Warner (D-Va.) have announced plans to introduce the AI-Related Job Impacts Clarity Act, a bipartisan bill aimed at increasing transparency regarding the impact of AI on employment. The proposed legislation would require major companies and federal agencies to submit quarterly reports to the Department of Labor detailing AI-related job effects, including layoffs and job displacement. The Department of Labor would then compile this data into a publicly available report for Congress and the public. Senator Hawley emphasized the need for Americans to understand how AI is affecting the workforce to ensure that AI serves the people, while Senator Warner highlighted the importance of obtaining accurate data to inform policies that promote opportunities without leaving workers behind.

National Strategy
[30 October 2025] GAIN AI Act introduced:
Chairman John Moolenaar and Ranking Member Raja Krishnamoorthi of the House Select Committee on China have introduced the Guaranteeing Access and Innovation for National Artificial Intelligence (GAIN AI) Act, aiming to prioritize American companies in the allocation of advanced AI chips. This legislation seeks to ensure that US firms are given precedence over foreign adversaries, particularly those from arms-embargoed countries, in acquiring these critical technologies. For background, the GAIN AI Act was previously introduced as part of the National Defense Authorization Act, as a more direct successor to the recently rescinded Biden-era AI Diffusion Rule to address ongoing national security and supply chain concerns regarding advanced AI chips. The GAIN AI Act is now being introduced as standalone legislation rather than as an amendment to the National Defense Authorization Act.

[28 October 2025] GUARD Act introduced:
US senators Mark R. Warner (D-VA), Josh Hawley (R-MO), Richard Blumenthal (D-CT), Chris Murphy (D-CT), and Katie Britt (R-AL) have introduced the GUARD Act, a bipartisan bill aimed at protecting minors from the potential dangers of AI chatbots. The legislation seeks to ban AI companions for minors, require AI chatbots to disclose their non-human status, and establish new criminal offenses for companies that develop AI targeting minors with solicitations or production of sexual content.

The bill seeks to empower parents to protect their children from potential harm as AI becomes increasingly integrated into education. The bill has received support from organizations like Parents Defending Education Action, which emphasizes the importance of protecting students' privacy and reinforcing parental rights under existing laws.

The legislation highlights AI applications such as planning for unexpected events, protecting data and intellectual property, and enhancing cybersecurity and regulatory compliance. Additionally, the SBA is tasked with conducting outreach to understand small businesses' concerns regarding AI adoption.

[15 October 2025] US federal AI regulation is on the way, says US senator:
It is reported that at a CNBC AI Summit, Senator Marsha Blackburn (R-Tenn.) has announced that federal regulation of AI is forthcoming in the US amid growing consensus among lawmakers on the need to establish guidelines to govern AI technologies, ensuring they are developed and deployed responsibly. Senator Blackburn has been pushing multiple legislative fronts beyond the Kids Online Safety Act, working on an online consumer privacy protection bill that would let users "set those firewalls and protect the virtual you," plus legislation targeting how AI systems use someone's name, image, or likeness without consent. “The reason the states have stepped in, whether it’s to protect consumers or protect children, is because the federal government has, to date, not been able to pass any federal preemptive legislation,” Senator Blackburn said. “We have to have the states standing in the gap until such time that Congress will say no to the big tech platforms."

[30 September 2025] AI LEAD Act introduced:
US senators Josh Hawley (R-Mo.) and Dick Durbin (D-Ill.) have introduced the bipartisan "Aligning Incentives for Leadership, Excellence, and Advancement in Development (AI LEAD) Act", which proposes to: (1) classify AI systems as products, allowing for product liability claims to be brought against companies if their AI system causes harm; (2) incentivize AI companies to design their systems safely rather than rushing AI systems to market; and (3) hold AI companies accountable without stifling innovation by allowing for the continued development of beneficial AI systems.

Generative AI
[30 September 2025] CAISI releases evaluation of DeepSeek AI models:
The US Center for AI Standards and Innovation (CAISI) has published its evaluation of AI models from China's DeepSeek and found they lag behind US models in performance, cost, security, and adoption. The assessment revealed that DeepSeek's models are more susceptible to security vulnerabilities, including agent hijacking and jailbreaking attacks, and tend to echo inaccurate narratives aligned with the Chinese Communist Party. The evaluation from CAISI was in response to President Trump’s America’s AI Action Plan, which directed CAISI to conduct research and publish evaluations of frontier models from China.

The OSTP is requesting comments on regulations across all sectors, including health care, where the underlying assumptions, technical requirements or compliance frameworks may create unnecessary barriers to beneficial AI applications. The consultation closes on 27 October 2025.

[10 September 2025] SANDBOX Act introduced:
US Senator Ted Cruz (R-Texas) has introduced a bill titled the "Strengthening Artificial intelligence Normalization and Diffusion By Oversight and eXperimentation", (SANDBOX Act), aimed at reducing regulatory burdens on AI development by establishing a "sandbox" environment. This initiative seeks to provide AI developers with a controlled space to innovate without the immediate constraints of existing regulations. The Office of Science and Technology Policy (OSTP) would coordinate across federal agencies to evaluate requests under their purview. Congress would collect regular reports on how often rules were waived or modified to better inform future policy decisions and the regulatory structure applicable to AI.

Generative AI
[4 September 2025] CHAT Act introduced:
The Children Harmed by AI Technology Act (CHAT Act/SB 2714) has been introduced in the Senate. Covered entities, defined as entities that own, operate, or otherwise make available a companion AI chatbot to individuals in the United States, must verify the age of individuals creating new accounts by requesting age information and confirming it through a reliable commercial method. If the user is a minor, the account must be linked to a verified parental account, parental consent must be obtained before use, parents must be notified immediately if the chatbot detects suicidal ideation, and minors must be blocked from accessing chatbots that engage in sexually explicit communication. Age verification data may only be collected, processed, and stored to the extent strictly necessary for verification, parental consent, or compliance purposes. Chatbot interactions must be monitored for signs of suicidal ideation, with appropriate resources, including contact information for the National Suicide Prevention Lifeline, provided to the user and the parent when relevant.

Online Safety & Child Protection
Data Privacy & Protection
Health & Life Sciences
[22 August 2025] The No Advanced Chips for the Chinese Communist Party (CCP) Act introduced:
House of Representatives member Raja Krishnamoorthi (D-IL-8) has introduced The No Advanced Chips for the Chinese Communist Party (CCP) Act to the House of Representatives. The Act prohibits exports of advanced AI semiconductors to China unless both the US Department of Commerce and the United States Congress approve. It also provides that semiconductors are covered if they exceed thresholds for processing power, dynamic random-access memory (DRAM) bandwidth, or interconnect capacity. The Act requires the Secretary of Commerce to conduct an interagency review with the Secretary of Defence, the Secretary of Energy, the Secretary of State, and the Director of National Intelligence. The Act requires assessment of risks to US national security, technological leadership, military applications, human rights, and economic impacts following the enactment of a joint resolution by Congress approving each export. The Act provides exemptions for humanitarian purposes, US diplomatic operations, and chip repairs.

Defense & National Security
[5 August 2025] US House of Representatives introduce bill to codify executive order to preventing woke AI in the Federal Government:
House of Representatives member Jimmy Patronis (R-FL-1) has introduced to a bill (H.R.4873) to codify Executive Order 14319 on public procurement access for AI in the federal government. The bill would give Executive Order 14319 the effect of the law. The order requires the Director of the Office of Management and Budget (OMB), in conjunction with the Administrator for Federal Procurement Policy, the Administrator of General Services, and the Director of the Office of Science and Technology Policy, to issue guidance ensuring that agency heads procure only those large language models (LLMs) developed in accordance with the principles of truth-seeking and ideological neutrality. It also requires each agency head to include section terms in government contracts with LLMs requiring that the procured LLM comply with the principles of truth-seeking and ideological neutrality. This must be done for each contract entered into following the date of publication of the OMB guidance. It must also be done for existing contracts to the greatest extent possible. Additionally, within 90 days of the issuance of OMB guidance, agency heads must adopt procedures to ensure that procured LLMs comply with the principles. Executive Order 14319 includes a standard disclaimer stating that it does not create any legally enforceable rights or benefits against the US government, its agencies, employees, or other individuals. The bill would make Executive Order 14319 legally enforceable if adopted.

Cybersecurity
[1 August 2025] Senate revisits VET AI Act:
Senators John Hickenlooper (D-Colo) and Shelley Moore Capito, (R-W.V) have reintroduced the Validation and Evaluation for Trustworthy Artificial Intelligence (VET AI) Act. The bill passed out of the Senate Commerce, Science, and Transportation Committee in 2024 before the end of the 118th Congress. The bill would require National Institute of Standards and Technology to lead the development of “detailed specifications, guidelines, and recommendations for third-party evaluators to work with AI companies to provide robust independent external assurance and verification” of AI systems. The bill also includes provisions to develop voluntary, consensus-based guidelines for internal and external assurance of AI systems. The bill applies to developers, deployers, and independent evaluators of AI, aiming to enhance trust, accountability, and risk management without mandating specific technologies. It establishes an advisory committee to recommend qualifications for AI assurance professionals and requires the Secretary of Commerce to study the sector’s capabilities and report to Congress.

Data Privacy & Protection
[24 July 2025] President Trump dismisses copyright concerns and state AI laws in AI Action Plan announcement speech:
During his speech announcing the US AI Action Plan at the Winning the AI Race Summit in Washington DC, President Trump (1) expressed that the US’ stance on intellectual property and AI would be a "commonsense application" that does not force AI companies to pay for each piece of copyrighted material used in training frontier models - "You can't be expected to have a successful AI program when every single article, book, or anything else that you've read or studied, you're supposed to pay for...We appreciate, but just can't do it - because it's not doable" (Trump); and (2) echo tech sector complaints about state AI laws creating a patchwork of regulation - "You can’t have one state holding you up...We need one common sense federal standard that supersedes all states, supersedes everybody" (Trump).

Copyright & IP
[24 July 2025] TRAIN Act introduced:
US senator Senator Peter Welch (D-Vt.) has re-introduced the Transparency and Responsibility for Artificial Intelligence Networks (TRAIN) Act (SB 2455) in the US Senate. A previous version of the bill (S.5379) was introduced in November 2024. The bill establishes a subpoena mechanism allowing copyright holders to determine whether their works were used to train generative AI models. The bill applies to developers, including entities and government agencies who design, substantially modify, or train such models, excluding non-commercial end users. It permits copyright owners to request subpoenas from district courts to compel developers to disclose records or copies of training material, provided there is a good faith belief that their copyrighted content was used. Developers must comply promptly and maintain confidentiality, while noncompliance creates a rebuttable presumption of infringement.

Copyright & IP
Generative AI
[23 July 2025] US AI Action Plan unveiled:
The US White Office has unveiled the US AI Action Plan in accordance with President Trump’s January executive order on Removing Barriers to American Leadership in AI. The Plan identifies over 90 Federal policy actions across three pillars – Accelerating Innovation, Building American AI Infrastructure, and Leading in International Diplomacy and Security – that the Trump Administration will take in the coming weeks and months. Key policies in the AI Action Plan include: (1) Exporting American AI: The Commerce and State Departments will partner with industry to deliver secure, full-stack AI export packages – including hardware, models, software, applications, and standards – to America’s friends and allies around the world; (2) Promoting Rapid Buildout of Data Centers: Expediting and modernizing permits for data centers and semiconductor fabs, as well as creating new national initiatives to increase high-demand occupations like electricians and HVAC technicians; (3) Enabling Innovation and Adoption: Removing onerous Federal regulations that hinder AI development and deployment, and seek private sector input on rules to remove; and (4) Upholding Free Speech in Frontier Models: Updating Federal procurement guidelines to ensure that the government only contracts with frontier large language model developers who ensure that their systems are objective and free from top-down ideological bias.

National Strategy
[21 July 2025] Trump to unveil 3 AI executive orders in lead-up to sweeping action plan:
It is reported, according to inside anonymous sources, that President Trump plans to sign three AI-focused executive orders in the runup to the release of the administration’s sweeping AI Action Plan anticipated on 23 July. The orders are expected to be signed either on 22 July or before the White House’s AI Action Plan event kicks off on 23 July. Each order focuses on one of three aspects of AI regulation and policy that the administration has prioritized: (1) the first order will spearhead AI-ready infrastructure by addressing energy and permitting issues associated with data centers and the computational demands of running AI applications, and stipulating the Department of Energy issue requests for proposals for new data centers at three Department of Energy sites; (2) the second order will establish and promote a US technology export regime by accelerating the export of US-made AI technologies alongside the US International Development Finance Corporation and Export–Import Bank of the United States and seeking to further enable global diffusion of the US-made AI software buildouts; and (3) the third order will ensure large language models are not generating “woke” or otherwise biased information (particularly within AI tools procured by the federal government).

Generative AI
[21 July 2025] Artificial Intelligence Accountability and Personal Data Protection Act introduced:
US senators Josh Hawley (R-MO) and Richard Blumenthal (D-CT) have introduced to the Senate the Artificial Intelligence Accountability and Personal Data Protection Act (Bill No. S.2367). The Act seeks to establish a federal tort against the appropriation, collection, processing, sale, or other exploitation of individuals’ data without express, prior consent. The Act applies to entities, including those deploying generative AI systems, that misuse personal or inferred data, and provides individuals with a private right of action to claim damages, injunctive relief, and attorney’s fees. It bans predispute arbitration agreements and class action waivers, mandates explicit consent with clear third-party disclosure, and sets a federal minimum standard without pre-empting stronger state protections.

[1 July 2025] US Senate strikes AI regulation ban from Budget Reconciliation Bill:
It is reported that the US Senate voted overwhelmingly (99-1) to remove a 10-year federal moratorium on state regulation of AI from the Budget Reconciliation Bill (aka Trump's "Big Beautiful Bill"). Senator Marsha Blackburn (R.-Tenn) initially proposed an amendment that would reduce the moratorium to 5 years and allow states to regulate issues like protecting artists' voices or child online safety if they do not impose an "undue or disproportionate burden" on AI. However, senator Blackburn withdrew her support for the amendment before the vote.

[30 June 2025] US Senate debates whether to adopt revised state AI regulation ban:
It is reported that US senators Marsha Blackburn (R.-Tenn) and Ted Cruz (R.-Texas) have agreed to a revised federal moratorium on state regulation of AI to five years and allow states to adopt rules on child online safety and protecting artists' image or likeliness. For context, senator Cruz originally proposed securing compliance by blocking states that regulate AI from a $42 billion broadband infrastructure fund as part of a broad tax and budget bill. However, under a revised version proposed by senator Blackburn, the moratorium would (1) be cut down to 5 years; (2) allow states to regulate issues like protecting artists' voices or child online safety if they do not impose an "undue or disproportionate burden" on AI; and (3) only restricts states from tapping a new $500 million fund to support AI infrastructure.

Online Safety & Child Protection
Copyright & IP
[25 June 2025] "No Adversarial AI Act" introduced:
A bipartisan group of US lawmakers have introduced a bill dubbed the "No Adversarial AI Act" in both houses of Congress that would bar US executive agencies from using AI models developed in China, including those from DeepSeek. The bill would (1) create a permanent framework for barring the use of all Chinese AI models from U.S. executive agencies, as well as those from Russia, Iran and North Korea; (2) require the Federal Acquisition Security Council to create a list of AI models developed in those countries and regularly update it; (3) restrict US federal agencies from buying or using those AI technologies without an exemption, such as for carrying out research, from the US Congress or the Office of Management and Budget; and (4) contain a provision that can be used to get technologies off the list with proof that they are not controlled or influenced by a foreign adversary of the US.

Defense & National Security
Cybersecurity
Generative AI
[21 June 2025] Senate parliamentarian approves state AI law moratorium in Budget Reconciliation Bill:
The Senate’s parliamentarian (i.e. rules referee) has allowed the Republicans to include in the Budget Reconciliation Bill a 10-year moratorium on enforcing state and local AI laws. For background, Senate Commerce Chair Ted Cruz (R-Texas) rewrote a House-passed AI moratorium to try to comply with the chamber’s budgetary rules. Senator Cruz's version tied compliance with the moratorium to eligibility for Broadband Equity, Access, and Deployment (BEAD) program funding—a $42 billion federal broadband investment. Both parties made their arguments before the parliamentarian on 19 June. On 21 June, the Senate Parliamentarian determined that the proposed moratorium survived procedural scrutiny — known as the Byrd Rule — and will remain in the bill. This ruling effectively shields the moratorium provision from a filibuster hurdle and allows it to advance through the budget reconciliation process with a simple majority vote. The path forward for the AI moratorium is now procedurally clear, pending broader negotiations over the bill’s contents.

[18 June 2025] Preventing Deep Fake Scams Act introduced:
US senator Jon Husted (R-Ohio) has introduced the Preventing Deep Fake Scams Act. This bill would establish a task force to assess how financial institutions can use AI to protect against fraud and identify potential risks associated with abusing AI technology, particularly deepfakes. It would also establish best practices to safeguard consumers from these types of financial crimes. The task force would produce a detailed report of its findings to Congress within one year of the bill’s enactment.

Cybersecurity
[12 June 2025] Responsible Innovation and Safe Expertise (RISE) Act introduced:
US senator Cynthia Lummis (R-WY) has introduced the Responsible Innovation and Safe Expertise (RISE) Act of 2025, clarifying that when professionals—such as physicians, attorneys, engineers, and financial advisors—use AI systems in their practice, they retain the legal duty to exercise due diligence, verify the system’s outputs, and stand behind the advice they deliver. This is the first targeted liability reform legislation for professional-grade AI. Developers may claim safe-harbor immunity only if they publicly release a model card and key design specifications so that physicians, attorneys, engineers, and other professionals can understand what the AI can and cannot do before relying on it for decisions that affect end users.

Generative AI
Health & Life Sciences
[12 June 2025] H. R. 3961 bill introduced to prohibit the Secretary of Defence from entering into software source code contracts with entities with certain relationships with China:
A bill to prohibit the Secretary of Defence from entering into software source code contracts with entities with certain relationships with China, and for other purposes has been introduced to the House of Representatives. The Bill prohibits the Department of Defence from entering into, renewing, or extending software source code contracts with entities that have certain ties to China. The Bill applies to software and AI development firms that either operate or have significant interests in Chinese AI research facilities, have granted China access to source code that could enable reverse engineering, or are connected to data centres in China handling such code. A waiver may be granted if the Secretary of Defence determines it to be in the national security interest of the US. The prohibition will apply to relevant contracts entered into within three years of the Bill’s enactment.

Cybersecurity
[11 June 2025] White House AI Czar David Sacks warns against overregulating AI:
It is reported that White House AI Czar David Sacks spoke at AWS Summit in Washington, warning that the US is at risk of overregulating artificial intelligence, with hundreds of bills currently making their way through state legislatures. Sacks cautioned that premature or excessive regulation could stifle innovation before the full potential and risks of AI are even understood, potentially harming the economy by “killing this thing in the cradle”. Sacks also downplayed the risk that coveted US AI chips could be smuggled to bad actors, and expressed concern that regulating AI too tightly could stifle growth and cede the critical market to China.

[6 June 2025] AI Training Extension Act introduced:
House of Representatives member Nancy Mace (SC-1) has reintroduced the AI Training Extension Act of 2025, a bipartisan bill designed to modernize the federal workforce by enhancing access to AI training. The legislation aims to ensure that federal agencies are equipped to adapt to the evolving landscape of AI, incorporating practical use cases, privacy safeguards, and security measures into the training. Additionally, it seeks to align training with government-wide standards set by the Office of Management and Budget (OMB), ultimately strengthening the government's capacity to utilize AI in an informed, ethical, and efficient manner across various departments and services.

Cybersecurity
[5 June 2025] Senate proposes alternative to AI moratorium in Trump’s ‘big, beautiful bill’:
The Senate Commerce Committee has proposed an alternative to a controversial provision in the Budget Reconciliation Bill that would have imposed a decade-long ban on state regulation of AI. Instead, the Senate version ties access to federal broadband funding to states refraining from regulating AI, rather than outright prohibiting state oversight. This move comes after bipartisan concerns that a blanket ban would undermine states’ ability to address the evolving risks of AI, such as deepfakes and misinformation, while tech industry leaders argue that a patchwork of state laws could stifle innovation. The revised Senate proposal has sparked debate among lawmakers and digital safety advocates, and its fate remains uncertain as it must comply with Senate procedural rules and address the differing priorities of both parties.

Online Safety & Child Protection
[3 June 2025] US AI Safety Institute rebranded to the US Center for AI Standards and Innovation:
Under the direction of President Trump, the US Secretary of Commerce has announced plans to reform the US AI Safety Institute into the Center for AI Standards and Innovation (CAISI). CAISI will continue to operate within NIST and regularly collaborate and coordinate with other organisations within NIST (including the Information Technology Laboratory) as well as other bureaus within the Department of Commerce (including BIS), focusing on (1) testing and collaborative research to leverage and secure commercial AI; (2) developing guidelines and voluntary standards with NIST; (3) establishing agreements with private sector AI developers for unclassified evaluations of national security risks (like cybersecurity and biosecurity); and (4) assessing both US and adversary AI capabilities, including potential vulnerabilities and foreign influence.

Cybersecurity
[23 May 2025] President Trump signs executive orders on nuclear energy, supporting AI:
US President Trump has signed four executive orders aimed at easing regulations on and expanding the production of nuclear energy. The executive orders aim to: (1) reform nuclear energy research at the Department of Energy; (2) clear a path to allow the Energy Department to build nuclear reactors on federally-owned land; (3) overhaul the Nuclear Regulatory Commission; and (4) expand uranium mining and enrichment in the US. Together, the orders intend to spur a "nuclear energy renaissance" through the construction of new reactors that would satisfy the electricity demands of data centres for AI and other emerging industries.

[16 May 2025] US state attorneys oppose 10 year state-based AI regulation moratorium:
A number of state attorneys general, both Republican and Democratic, have signed a letter urging Congress to reject an amendment in the Budget Reconciliation Bill that would impose a 10 year moratorium on state-level AI regulation. The letter warns that (1) "the impact of such a broad moratorium would be sweeping and wholly destructive of reasonable state efforts to prevent known harms associated with AI"; and (2) "this bill will affect hundreds of existing and pending state laws passed and considered by both Republican and Democratic state legislatures."

Generative AI
[13 May 2025] BIS issues policy statement clarifying export controls on AI related integrated circuits:
The US Bureau of Industry and Security (BIS) has issued a policy statement clarifying that certain activities involving advanced computing integrated circuits (ICs) and related commodities used to train AI models may require an export authorisation under the Export Administration Regulations. These activities are: (1) exports, reexports, or transfers (in-country) of advanced computing ICs and commodities subject to the EAR to any party, such as foreign Infrastructure as a Service (IaaS) providers (e.g., data center providers), when the exporter, reexporter, or transferor has “knowledge” that the IaaS provider will use these items to conduct training of AI models for or on behalf of parties headquartered in D:5 countries (including China) or Macau; (2) transfers (in-country), defined as a change in end use or end user, of advanced computing ICs and commodities subject to the EAR already in the possession of parties such as IaaS providers, if there is “knowledge” that the items will be used by the transferee to train AI models for or on behalf of parties headquartered in D:5 countries (including China) or Macau; and (3) a “U.S. person” provides any ‘support’ or performs any contract, service, or employment, when there is “knowledge” such activity will be used for or may assist the training of AI models for or on behalf of parties headquartered in D:5 countries (including China) or Macau.

Defense & National Security
[12 May 2025] US House Committee proposes 10-year ban on state AI regulations:
The House Republicans on the US House Energy and Commerce Committee has announced the budget reconciliation bill that includes the Artificial Intelligence and Information Technology Modernisation initiative. The bill applies to developers and providers of AI models, systems, and automated decision systems. Notably it proposes a 10-year federal moratorium on the enforcement of state and local laws regulating such technologies, which would effectively block substantive state rules on AI transparency and bias audits until 2035. Exceptions are made for laws that facilitate AI deployment, streamline administrative procedures, or impose neutral, cost-based requirements comparable to those applied to non-AI systems.

Agentic AI
Data Privacy & Protection
[12 May 2025] Trump administration officially rescinds Biden’s AI diffusion rules:
The US Department of Commerce (DOC) has initiated a rescission of the Biden Administration’s AI Diffusion Rule which was set to come into force on 15 May to limit the export of US-made AI chips to other countries. The Bureau of Industry and Security (BIS) plans to publish a Federal Register notice formalizing the rescission and will issue a replacement rule in the future. In the meantime, the Secretary of Commerce for Industry and Security Jeffery Kessler has instructed the BIS enforcement officials not to enforce the Biden Administration’s AI Diffusion Rule. The BIS has further announced actions to strengthen export controls for overseas AI chips, including: (1) "issuing guidance that using Huawei Ascend chips anywhere in the world violates US export controls"; (2) "issuing guidance warning the public about the potential consequences of allowing US AI chips to be used for training and interference of Chinese AI models"; and (3) "issuing guidance to U.S. companies on how to protect supply chains against diversion tactics".

[8 May 2025] Chip Security Act introduced:
US senator Tom Cotton (R-Arkansas) has introduced the Chip Security Act, aimed at preventing advanced US chips from being diverted to adversaries such as China. The legislation seeks to enhance oversight of advanced chips and directs the Commerce Department and Department of Defense to explore chip security mechanisms.

Cybersecurity
[6 May 2025] US lawmaker targets Nvidia chip smuggling to China with new bill:
It is reported that a US House of Representative member Bill Foster (D-Illinois) plans to introduce legislation in coming weeks (with existing bipartisan support) to verify the location of AI chips like those made by Nvidia after they are sold. The bill aims to address reports of widespread smuggling of Nvidia's chips into China in violation of US export control laws. The legislative text of the bill has yet to be revealed.

Defense & National Security
[30 April 2025] Trump officials plan changes to Biden's AI chip export rule:
It is reported that according to internal sources, the Trump administration is working on changes to a Biden-era 'Framework for Artificial Intelligence Diffusion' rule which is set to go into effect on 15 May 2025 to limit global access to AI chips under a three-tiered system. The sources suggest that the administrarion is planning to remove the tiered system, and replacing it with a global licensing regime with government-to-government agreements to regulate access to US AI chips (potentially as a negotiating tool in trade talks). The plans are still under discussion and subject to change.

Defense & National Security
[28 April 2025] House passes Take it Down Act, now proceeding to President Trump to sign into law:
The US House of Representatives has passed the bipartisan bill titled the Take it Down Act which is aimed at combating deepfake pornography, with a significant 409-2 vote. President Donald Trump is expected to sign the measure into law, which is sponsored in the Senate by Ted Cruz and Amy Klobuchar, and has received support from first lady Melania Trump.
